			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Renaissance</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the European Renaissance of the 14th–17th centuries. For the earlier European Renaissance, see <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Renaissance (disambiguation)">Renaissance (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg/220px-Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg" width="220" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg/330px-Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg/440px-Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2388" data-file-height="3824" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_David_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> (<a href="/wiki/Accademia_di_Belle_Arti_di_Firenze" title="Accademia di Belle Arti di Firenze">Accademia di Belle Arti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>) is a masterpiece of Renaissance and world art.</div>

<p>The <b>Renaissance</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">period</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">European history</a>, from the 14th to the 17th century, regarded as the cultural bridge between the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">modern history</a>. It started as a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_movement" title="Cultural movement">cultural movement</a> in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval period">Medieval period</a> and later spread to the rest of Europe, marking the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Modern_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern age">Modern age</a>.</p>
<p>The intellectual basis of the Renaissance was its own invented version of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanism</a>, derived from the concept of Roman <a href="/wiki/Humanitas" title="Humanitas">Humanitas</a> and the rediscovery of classical Greek philosophy, such as that of <a href="/wiki/Protagoras" title="Protagoras">Protagoras</a>, who said that "Man is the measure of all things." This new thinking became manifest in art, architecture, politics, science and literature. Early examples were the development of <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(graphical)" title="Perspective (graphical)">perspective</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oil_painting" title="Oil painting">oil painting</a> and the recycled knowledge of how to make <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>. Although the invention of <a href="/wiki/Movable_type#Metal_movable_type_in_Europe" title="Movable type">metal movable type</a> sped the dissemination of ideas from the later 15th century, the changes of the Renaissance were not uniformly experienced across Europe.</p>
<p>As a cultural movement, the Renaissance encompassed innovative flowering of Latin and vernacular literatures, beginning with the 14th-century resurgence of learning based on classical sources, which contemporaries credited to <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a>; the development of linear perspective and other techniques of rendering a more natural reality in <a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">painting</a>; and gradual but widespread <a href="/wiki/History_of_education" title="History of education">educational reform</a>. In politics, the Renaissance contributed to the development of the customs and conventions of <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomacy</a>, and in science to an increased reliance on observation and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>. Although the Renaissance saw revolutions in many intellectual pursuits, as well as social and political upheaval, it is perhaps best known for its artistic developments and the contributions of such <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymaths</a> as <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, who inspired the term "Renaissance man".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Renaissance began in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, in the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Various theories have been proposed to account for its origins and characteristics, focusing on a variety of factors including the social and civic peculiarities of Florence at the time: its political structure; the patronage of its dominant family, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-strathern_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strathern-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and the migration of <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Greek scholars</a> and texts to Italy following the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Ottoman Turks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica1_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harris_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norwich_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich-9">[9]</a></sup> Other major centres were northern <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">Italian city-states</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Milan" title="Duchy of Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, and finally <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Papacy" title="Renaissance Papacy">Renaissance Papacy</a>.</p>
<p>The Renaissance has a long and complex <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a>, and, in line with general scepticism of discrete <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodizations</a>, there has been much debate among historians reacting to the 19th-century glorification of the "Renaissance" and individual culture heroes as "Renaissance men", questioning the usefulness of <i>Renaissance</i> as a term and as a historical delineation.<sup id="cite_ref-brotton_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brotton-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Art_history" title="Art history">art historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Panofsky" title="Erwin Panofsky">Erwin Panofsky</a> observed of this resistance to the concept of "Renaissance":</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It is perhaps no accident that the factuality of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> has been most vigorously questioned by those who are not obliged to take a professional interest in the aesthetic aspects of civilization—historians of economic and social developments, political and religious situations, and, most particularly, natural science—but only exceptionally by students of literature and hardly ever by historians of Art.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Some observers have called into question whether the Renaissance was a cultural "advance" from the Middle Ages, instead seeing it as a period of pessimism and <a href="/wiki/Nostalgia" title="Nostalgia">nostalgia</a> for <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-huizinga_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huizinga-12">[12]</a></sup> while social and economic historians, especially of the <i><a href="/wiki/Longue_dur%C3%A9e" title="Longue durée">longue durée</a></i>, have instead focused on the continuity between the two eras,<sup id="cite_ref-starn_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starn-13">[13]</a></sup> which are linked, as Panofsky observed, "by a thousand ties".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The word <i>Renaissance</i>, literally meaning "Rebirth" in French, first appeared in English in the 1830s.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The word also occurs in <a href="/wiki/Jules_Michelet" title="Jules Michelet">Jules Michelet</a>'s 1855 work, <i>Histoire de France</i>. The word <i>Renaissance</i> has also been extended to other historical and cultural movements, such as the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mur_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mur-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Origins"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Origins</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>
<p>The Renaissance was a cultural movement that profoundly affected European intellectual life in the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>. Beginning in Italy, and spreading to the rest of Europe by the 16th century, its influence was felt in literature, philosophy, art, music, politics, science, religion, and other aspects of intellectual inquiry. Renaissance scholars employed the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanist</a> method in study, and searched for realism and human emotion in art.<sup id="cite_ref-perry-humanities_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perry-humanities-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance humanist">Renaissance humanists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Poggio_Bracciolini" title="Poggio Bracciolini">Poggio Bracciolini</a> sought out in Europe's monastic libraries the Latin literary, historical, and oratorical texts of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Antiquity">Antiquity</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> (1453) generated a wave of émigré Greek scholars bringing precious manuscripts in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a>, many of which had fallen into obscurity in the West. It is in their new focus on literary and historical texts that Renaissance scholars differed so markedly from the medieval scholars of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>, who had focused on studying <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> works of natural sciences, philosophy and mathematics, rather than on such cultural texts.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_(Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_%28Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_%28Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_%28Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_%28Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_%28Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_%28Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4001" data-file-height="6026" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sandro_Botticelli_-_Idealized_Portrait_of_a_Lady_(Portrait_of_Simonetta_Vespucci_as_Nymph)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_a_Young_Woman_(Botticelli,_Frankfurt)" title="Portrait of a Young Woman (Botticelli, Frankfurt)">Portrait of a Young Woman</a></i> (c. 1480-85) (<a href="/wiki/Simonetta_Vespucci" title="Simonetta Vespucci">Simonetta Vespucci</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the revival of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Platonism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Platonism">neo-Platonism</a> Renaissance humanists did not reject <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>; quite the contrary, many of the Renaissance's greatest works were devoted to it, and the Church patronized many works of Renaissance art. However, a subtle shift took place in the way that intellectuals approached religion that was reflected in many other areas of cultural life.<sup id="cite_ref-openuni_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openuni-18">[18]</a></sup> In addition, many Greek Christian works, including the Greek New Testament, were brought back from Byzantium to Western Europe and engaged Western scholars for the first time since late antiquity. This new engagement with Greek Christian works, and particularly the return to the original Greek of the New Testament promoted by humanists <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">Lorenzo Valla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, would help pave the way for the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>.</p>
<p>Well after the first artistic return to classicism had been exemplified in the sculpture of <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Pisano" title="Nicola Pisano">Nicola Pisano</a>, Florentine painters led by <a href="/wiki/Masaccio" title="Masaccio">Masaccio</a> strove to portray the human form realistically, developing techniques to render <a href="/wiki/Linear_perspective" class="mw-redirect" title="Linear perspective">perspective</a> and light more naturally. <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">Political philosophers</a>, most famously <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a>, sought to describe political life as it really was, that is to understand it rationally. A critical contribution to Italian Renaissance humanism <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pico_della_Mirandola" title="Giovanni Pico della Mirandola">Giovanni Pico della Mirandola</a> wrote the famous text <i>"De hominis dignitate"</i> (<a href="/wiki/Oration_on_the_Dignity_of_Man" title="Oration on the Dignity of Man">Oration on the Dignity of Man</a>, 1486), which consists of a series of theses on philosophy, natural thought, faith and magic defended against any opponent on the grounds of reason. In addition to studying classical Latin and Greek, Renaissance authors also began increasingly to use <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> languages; combined with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">printing</a>, this would allow many more people access to books, especially the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In all, the Renaissance could be viewed as an attempt by intellectuals to study and improve the <a href="/wiki/Secular" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular">secular</a> and worldly, both through the revival of ideas from antiquity, and through novel approaches to thought. Some scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Stark" title="Rodney Stark">Rodney Stark</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> play down the Renaissance in favor of the earlier innovations of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">Italian city-states</a> in the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, which married responsive government, Christianity and the birth of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>. This analysis argues that, whereas the great European states (France and Spain) were absolutist monarchies, and others were under direct Church control, the independent city republics of Italy took over the principles of capitalism invented on monastic estates and set off a vast unprecedented commercial revolution that preceded and financed the Renaissance.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg/300px-Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg/450px-Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg/600px-Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1006" data-file-height="670" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sunset_over_florence_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, birthplace of the Renaissance</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many argue that the ideas characterizing the Renaissance had their origin in late 13th-century <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, in particular with the writings of <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a> (1265–1321) and <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> (1304–1374), as well as the paintings of <a href="/wiki/Giotto_di_Bondone" class="mw-redirect" title="Giotto di Bondone">Giotto di Bondone</a> (1267–1337). Some writers date the Renaissance quite precisely; one proposed starting point is 1401, when the rival geniuses <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Lorenzo Ghiberti">Lorenzo Ghiberti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a> competed for the contract to build the bronze doors for the Baptistery of the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a> (Ghiberti won).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Others see more general competition between artists and polymaths such as Brunelleschi, Ghiberti, <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Masaccio" title="Masaccio">Masaccio</a> for artistic commissions as sparking the creativity of the Renaissance. Yet it remains much debated why the Renaissance began in Italy, and why it began when it did. Accordingly, several theories have been put forward to explain its origins.</p>
<p>During the Renaissance, money and art went hand in hand. Artists depended entirely on patrons while the patrons needed money to foster artistic talent. Wealth was brought to Italy in the 14th, 15th, and 16th centuries by expanding trade into Asia and Europe. Silver mining in <a href="/wiki/County_of_Tyrol" title="County of Tyrol">Tyrol</a> increased the flow of money. Luxuries from the Eastern world, brought home during the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, increased the prosperity of Genoa and Venice.<sup id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_Society_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic_Society-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jules_Michelet" title="Jules Michelet">Jules Michelet</a> defined the 16th-century Renaissance in France as a period in Europe's cultural history that represented a break from the Middle Ages, creating a modern understanding of humanity and its place in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_and_Greek_phases_of_Renaissance_humanism">Latin and Greek phases of Renaissance humanism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transmission_of_the_Greek_Classics" title="Transmission of the Greek Classics">Transmission of the Greek Classics</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-too_few_opinions" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this article <b>may not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">include all significant viewpoints</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Renaissance&amp;action=edit">improve the article</a> or <a href="/wiki/Talk:Renaissance" title="Talk:Renaissance">discuss the issue</a>.</span> <small><i>(June 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salutati.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Salutati.jpg/170px-Salutati.jpg" width="170" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Salutati.jpg/255px-Salutati.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Salutati.jpg/340px-Salutati.jpg 2x" data-file-width="652" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salutati.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Coluccio_Salutati" title="Coluccio Salutati">Coluccio Salutati</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In stark contrast to the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, when Latin scholars focused almost entirely on studying Greek and Arabic works of natural science, philosophy and mathematics,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Renaissance scholars were most interested in recovering and studying Latin and Greek literary, historical, and oratorical texts. Broadly speaking, this began in the 14th century with a Latin phase, when Renaissance scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coluccio_Salutati" title="Coluccio Salutati">Coluccio Salutati</a> (1331–1406), <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_de%27_Niccoli" title="Niccolò de' Niccoli">Niccolò de' Niccoli</a> (1364–1437) and <a href="/wiki/Poggio_Bracciolini" title="Poggio Bracciolini">Poggio Bracciolini</a> (1380–1459) scoured the libraries of Europe in search of works by such Latin authors as <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> By the early 15th century, the bulk of such Latin literature had been recovered; the Greek phase of Renaissance humanism was under way, as Western European scholars turned to recovering ancient Greek literary, historical, oratorical and theological texts.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike with Latin texts, which had been preserved and studied in Western Europe since late antiquity, the study of ancient Greek texts was very limited in medieval Western Europe. Ancient Greek works on science, maths and philosophy had been studied since the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> in Western Europe and in the medieval Islamic world (normally in translation), but Greek literary, oratorical and historical works (such as Homer, the Greek dramatists, <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>) were not studied in either the Latin or medieval Islamic worlds; in the Middle Ages these sorts of texts were only studied by Byzantine scholars. One of the greatest achievements of Renaissance scholars was to bring this entire class of Greek cultural works back into Western Europe for the first time since late antiquity. Arab logicians had inherited Greek ideas after they had invaded and conquered <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Levant" title="Muslim conquest of the Levant">Levant</a>. Their translations and commentaries on these ideas worked their way through the Arab West into <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Iberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">Sicily</a>, which became important centers for this transmission of ideas. From the 11th to the 13th century, many schools dedicated to the translation of philosophical and scientific works from <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> were established in Iberia. Most notably the <a href="/wiki/Toledo_School_of_Translators" title="Toledo School of Translators">Toledo School of Translators</a>. This work of translation from Islamic culture, though largely unplanned and disorganized, constituted one of the greatest transmissions of ideas in history.<sup id="cite_ref-MP_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MP-27">[27]</a></sup> This movement to reintegrate the regular study of Greek literary, historical, oratorical and theological texts back into the Western European curriculum is usually dated to the 1396 invitation from Coluccio Salutati to the Byzantine diplomat and scholar <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Chrysoloras" title="Manuel Chrysoloras">Manuel Chrysoloras</a> (c.1355–1415) to teach Greek in Florence.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> This legacy was continued by a number of expatriate Greek scholars, from <a href="/wiki/Basilios_Bessarion" title="Basilios Bessarion">Basilios Bessarion</a> to <a href="/wiki/Leo_Allatius" title="Leo Allatius">Leo Allatius</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_political_structures_in_Italy">Social and political structures in Italy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Italy_1494_AD.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Italy_1494_AD.png/220px-Italy_1494_AD.png" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Italy_1494_AD.png/330px-Italy_1494_AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Italy_1494_AD.png/440px-Italy_1494_AD.png 2x" data-file-width="1261" data-file-height="1805" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Italy_1494_AD.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political map of the Italian <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">Peninsula</a> circa 1494</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The unique political structures of late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> have led some to theorize that its unusual social climate allowed the emergence of a rare cultural efflorescence. Italy did not exist as a <a href="/wiki/Nation-state" class="mw-redirect" title="Nation-state">political entity</a> in the early modern period. Instead, it was divided into smaller <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">city states</a> and territories: the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Naples" title="Kingdom of Naples">Kingdom of Naples</a> controlled the south, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic of Florence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a> at the center, the <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milanese</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoese</a> to the north and west respectively, and the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetians</a> to the east. Fifteenth-century Italy was one of the most <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanised</a> areas in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Many of its cities stood among the ruins of ancient Roman buildings; it seems likely that the classical nature of the Renaissance was linked to its origin in the Roman Empire's heartland.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian and political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Skinner" title="Quentin Skinner">Quentin Skinner</a> points out that <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Freising" title="Otto of Freising">Otto of Freising</a> (c. 1114–1158), a German bishop visiting north Italy during the 12th century, noticed a widespread new form of political and social organization, observing that Italy appeared to have exited from Feudalism so that its society was based on merchants and commerce. Linked to this was anti-monarchical thinking, represented in the famous early Renaissance fresco cycle <i>Allegory of Good and Bad Government in Siena</i> by <a href="/wiki/Ambrogio_Lorenzetti" title="Ambrogio Lorenzetti">Ambrogio Lorenzetti</a> (painted 1338–1340), whose strong message is about the virtues of fairness, justice, republicanism and good administration. Holding both Church and Empire at bay, these city republics were devoted to notions of liberty. Skinner reports that there were many defences of liberty such as the <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Palmieri" title="Matteo Palmieri">Matteo Palmieri</a> (1406–1475) celebration of Florentine genius not only in art, sculpture and architecture, but "the remarkable efflorescence of moral, social and political philosophy that occurred in Florence at the same time".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even cities and states beyond central Italy, such as the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic of Florence</a> at this time, were also notable for their merchant <a href="/wiki/Republics" class="mw-redirect" title="Republics">Republics</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>. Although in practice these were <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchical</a>, and bore little resemblance to a modern <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>, they did have democratic features and were responsive states, with forms of participation in governance and belief in liberty.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The relative political freedom they afforded was conducive to academic and artistic advancement.<sup id="cite_ref-burckhardt-republics_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burckhardt-republics-35">[35]</a></sup> Likewise, the position of Italian cities such as Venice as great trading centres made them intellectual crossroads. <a href="/wiki/Merchants" class="mw-redirect" title="Merchants">Merchants</a> brought with them ideas from far corners of the globe, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. Venice was Europe's gateway to trade with the East, and a producer of fine <a href="/wiki/Venetian_glass" title="Venetian glass">glass</a>, while Florence was a capital of textiles. The wealth such business brought to Italy meant large public and private artistic projects could be commissioned and individuals had more leisure time for study.<sup id="cite_ref-burckhardt-republics_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burckhardt-republics-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Black_Death/Plague"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_Death.2FPlague">Black Death/Plague</span></h3>
<p>One theory that has been advanced is that the devastation in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> caused by the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, which hit <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> between 1348 and 1350, resulted in a shift in the world view of people in 14th-century Italy. Italy was particularly badly hit by the plague, and it has been speculated that the resulting familiarity with death caused thinkers to dwell more on their lives on Earth, rather than on <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> It has also been argued that the Black Death prompted a new wave of piety, manifested in the <a href="/wiki/Patron#Arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Patron">sponsorship</a> of religious works of art.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> However, this does not fully explain why the Renaissance occurred specifically in Italy in the 14th century. The Black Death was a pandemic that affected all of Europe in the ways described, not only Italy. The Renaissance's emergence in Italy was most likely the result of the complex interaction of the above factors.<sup id="cite_ref-brotton_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brotton-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The plague was carried by fleas on sailing vessels returning from the ports of Asia, spreading quickly due to lack of proper sanitation: the population of England, then about 4.2 million, lost 1.4 million people to the bubonic plague. Florence's population was nearly halved in the year 1347. As a result of the decimation in the populace the value of the working class increased, and commoners came to enjoy more freedom. To answer the increased need for labor, workers traveled in search of the most favorable position economically.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The demographic decline due to the plague had economic consequences: the prices of food dropped and land values declined by 30 to 40% in most parts of Europe between 1350 and 1400.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Landholders faced a great loss, but for ordinary men and women it was a windfall. The survivors of the plague found not only that the prices of food were cheaper but also that lands were more abundant, and many of them inherited property from their dead relatives.</p>
<p>The spread of disease was significantly more rampant in areas of poverty. <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">Epidemics</a> ravaged cities, particularly children. Plagues were easily spread by lice, unsanitary drinking water, armies, or by poor sanitation. Children were hit the hardest because many diseases, such as typhus and syphilis, target the immune system, leaving young children without a fighting chance. Children in city dwellings were more affected by the spread of disease than the children of the wealthy.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Black Death caused greater upheaval to Florence's social and political structure than later epidemics. Despite a significant number of deaths among members of the ruling classes, the government of Florence continued to function during this period. Formal meetings of elected representatives were suspended during the height of the epidemic due to the chaotic conditions in the city, but a small group of officials was appointed to conduct the affairs of the city, which ensured continuity of government.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_conditions_in_Florence">Cultural conditions in Florence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg/170px-Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg/255px-Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg/340px-Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="1207" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lorenzo_de%27_Medici-ritratto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo de' Medici">Lorenzo de' Medici</a>, ruler of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> and patron of arts (Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Macchietti" title="Girolamo Macchietti">Girolamo Macchietti</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It has long been a matter of debate why the Renaissance began in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, and not elsewhere in Italy. Scholars have noted several features unique to Florentine cultural life that may have caused such a cultural movement. Many have emphasized the role played by the <a href="/wiki/Medici" class="mw-redirect" title="Medici">Medici</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Banking">banking family</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">ducal ruling house</a>, in patronizing and stimulating the arts. <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo de' Medici">Lorenzo de' Medici</a> (1449–1492) was the catalyst for an enormous amount of arts patronage, encouraging his countrymen to commission works from the leading artists of Florence, including <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo_Buonarroti" class="mw-redirect" title="Michelangelo Buonarroti">Michelangelo Buonarroti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-strathern_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strathern-5">[5]</a></sup> Works by <a href="/wiki/Neri_di_Bicci" title="Neri di Bicci">Neri di Bicci</a>, Botticelli, da Vinci and <a href="/wiki/Filippino_Lippi" title="Filippino Lippi">Filippino Lippi</a> had been commissioned additionally by the convent di San Donato agli Scopeti of the <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a> order in Florence.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Renaissance was certainly underway before Lorenzo de' Medici came to power – indeed, before the Medici family itself achieved hegemony in Florentine society. Some historians have postulated that Florence was the birthplace of the Renaissance as a result of luck, i.e. because "<a href="/wiki/Great_man_theory" title="Great man theory">Great Men</a>" were born there by chance:<sup id="cite_ref-burckhardt-individual_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burckhardt-individual-43">[43]</a></sup> Leonardo da Vinci, Botticelli and Michelangelo were all born in <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>. Arguing that such chance seems improbable, other historians have contended that these "Great Men" were only able to rise to prominence because of the prevailing cultural conditions at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanism">Humanism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pico1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Pico1.jpg/220px-Pico1.jpg" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Pico1.jpg/330px-Pico1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Pico1.jpg/440px-Pico1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1447" data-file-height="1897" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pico1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pico_della_Mirandola" class="mw-redirect" title="Pico della Mirandola">Pico della Mirandola</a> wrote the famous <i><a href="/wiki/Oration_on_the_Dignity_of_Man" title="Oration on the Dignity of Man">Oration on the Dignity of Man</a></i>, which has been called the "Manifesto of the Renaissance".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In some ways <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanism</a> was not a philosophy but a method of learning. In contrast to the medieval <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic</a> mode, which focused on resolving contradictions between authors, humanists would study ancient texts in the original and appraise them through a combination of reasoning and <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a>. Humanist education was based on the programme of 'Studia Humanitatis', the study of five humanities: <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moral_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Moral philosophy">moral philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>. Although historians have sometimes struggled to define humanism precisely, most have settled on "a middle of the road definition... the movement to recover, interpret, and assimilate the language, literature, learning and values of ancient Greece and Rome".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Above all, humanists asserted "the genius of man&#160;... the unique and extraordinary ability of the human mind".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humanist scholars shaped the intellectual landscape throughout the early modern period. Political philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a> revived the ideas of Greek and Roman thinkers and applied them in critiques of contemporary government. <a href="/wiki/Pico_della_Mirandola" class="mw-redirect" title="Pico della Mirandola">Pico della Mirandola</a> wrote the "manifesto" of the Renaissance, the <i><a href="/wiki/Oration_on_the_Dignity_of_Man" title="Oration on the Dignity of Man">Oration on the Dignity of Man</a></i>, a vibrant defence of thinking. <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Palmieri" title="Matteo Palmieri">Matteo Palmieri</a> (1406–1475), another humanist, is most known for his work <i>Della vita civile</i> ("On Civic Life"; printed 1528), which advocated <a href="/wiki/Classical_republicanism" title="Classical republicanism">civic humanism</a>, and for his influence in refining the <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuscan language">Tuscan vernacular</a> to the same level as Latin. Palmieri drew on Roman philosophers and theorists, especially <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, who, like Palmieri, lived an active public life as a citizen and official, as well as a theorist and philosopher and also <a href="/wiki/Quintilian" title="Quintilian">Quintilian</a>. Perhaps the most succinct expression of his perspective on humanism is in a 1465 poetic work <i>La città di vita</i>, but an earlier work, <i>Della vita civile</i> (On Civic Life), is more wide-ranging. Composed as a series of dialogues set in a country house in the Mugello countryside outside Florence during the plague of 1430, Palmieri expounds on the qualities of the ideal citizen. The dialogues include ideas about how children develop mentally and physically, how citizens can conduct themselves morally, how citizens and states can ensure probity in public life, and an important debate on the difference between that which is pragmatically useful and that which is honest.</p>
<p>The humanists believed that it is important to transcend to the afterlife with a perfect mind and body, which could be attained with education. The purpose of humanism was to create a universal man whose person combined intellectual and physical excellence and who was capable of functioning honorably in virtually any situation.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This ideology was referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">uomo universale</a></i>, an ancient Greco-Roman ideal. Education during the Renaissance was mainly composed of ancient literature and history as it was thought that the classics provided moral instruction and an intensive understanding of human behavior.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_art" title="Renaissance art">Renaissance art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance_painting" title="Italian Renaissance painting">Renaissance painting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_influences_on_Western_art" title="Islamic influences on Western art">Islamic influences on Western art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg/220px-Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg" width="220" height="339" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg/330px-Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg/440px-Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1818" data-file-height="2802" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The tomb of <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Renaissance art marks a cultural rebirth at the close of the Middle Ages and rise of the Modern world. One of the distinguishing features of Renaissance art was its development of highly realistic linear perspective. <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto di Bondone</a> (1267–1337) is credited with first treating a painting as a window into space, but it was not until the demonstrations of architect <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a> (1377–1446) and the subsequent writings of <a href="/wiki/Leon_Battista_Alberti" title="Leon Battista Alberti">Leon Battista Alberti</a> (1404–1472) that perspective was formalized as an artistic technique.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The development of perspective was part of a wider trend towards <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a> in the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Painters developed other techniques, studying light, shadow, and, famously in the case of <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_anatomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Human anatomy">human anatomy</a>. Underlying these changes in artistic method was a renewed desire to depict the beauty of nature and to unravel the axioms of <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>, with the works of Leonardo, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> representing artistic pinnacles that were much imitated by other artists.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Other notable artists include <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a>, working for the Medici in Florence, <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a>, another Florentine, and <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a> in Venice, among others.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2258" data-file-height="3070" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Da_Vinci_Vitruve_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Vitruvian_Man" title="Vitruvian Man">Vitruvian Man</a></i> (c. 1490) demonstrates the effect writers of Antiquity had on Renaissance thinkers. Based on the specifications in <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De architectura</a></i> (1st century BC), Leonardo tried to draw the perfectly proportioned man.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, a particularly vibrant artistic culture developed. The work of <a href="/wiki/Hugo_van_der_Goes" title="Hugo van der Goes">Hugo van der Goes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Eyck" title="Jan van Eyck">Jan van Eyck</a> was particularly influential on the development of painting in Italy, both technically with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Oil_paint" title="Oil paint">oil paint</a> and canvas, and stylistically in terms of naturalism in representation (see <i><a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance in the Netherlands">Renaissance in the Netherlands</a></i>). Later, the work of <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Brueghel_the_Elder" class="mw-redirect" title="Pieter Brueghel the Elder">Pieter Brueghel the Elder</a> would inspire artists to depict themes of everyday life.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In architecture, <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a> was foremost in studying the remains of ancient classical buildings. With rediscovered knowledge from the 1st-century writer <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> and the flourishing discipline of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, Brunelleschi formulated the Renaissance style that emulated and improved on classical forms. His major feat of engineering was building the dome of the <a href="/wiki/Duomo_of_Florence" class="mw-redirect" title="Duomo of Florence">Florence Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Another building demonstrating this style is the church of St. Andrew in <a href="/wiki/Mantua" title="Mantua">Mantua</a>, built by Alberti. The outstanding architectural work of the High Renaissance was the rebuilding of <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>, combining the skills of <a href="/wiki/Bramante" class="mw-redirect" title="Bramante">Bramante</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_da_Sangallo_the_Younger" title="Antonio da Sangallo the Younger">Sangallo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Maderno" title="Carlo Maderno">Maderno</a>.</p>
<p>During the Renaissance, architects aimed to use columns, pilasters, and <a href="/wiki/Entablatures" class="mw-redirect" title="Entablatures">entablatures</a> as an integrated system. The Roman orders types of columns are used: Tuscan, Doric, Ionic, Corinthian and Composite. These can either be structural, supporting an arcade or architrave, or purely decorative, set against a wall in the form of <a href="/wiki/Pilasters" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilasters">pilasters</a>. One of the first buildings to use pilasters as an integrated system was in the Old Sacristy (1421–1440) by Brunelleschi.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Arches, semi-circular or (in the <a href="/wiki/Mannerism" title="Mannerism">Mannerist</a> style) segmental, are often used in arcades, supported on piers or columns with capitals. There may be a section of entablature between the capital and the springing of the arch. Alberti was one of the first to use the arch on a monumental. Renaissance vaults do not have ribs; they are semi-circular or segmental and on a square plan, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_style" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic style">Gothic</a> vault, which is frequently rectangular.</p>
<p>Renaissance artists were not pagans, although they admired antiquity and kept some ideas and symbols of the medieval past. <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Pisano" title="Nicola Pisano">Nicola Pisano</a> (c. 1220–c. 1278) imitated classical forms by portraying scenes from the Bible. His <i>Annunciation</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Pisa_Baptistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Pisa Baptistry">Baptistry at Pisa</a>, demonstrates that classical models influenced Italian art before the Renaissance took root as a literary movement <sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_in_the_Renaissance" title="History of science in the Renaissance">History of science in the Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_technology" title="Renaissance technology">Renaissance technology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacioli.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pacioli.jpg/170px-Pacioli.jpg" width="170" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pacioli.jpg/255px-Pacioli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pacioli.jpg/340px-Pacioli.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1250" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacioli.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_Luca_Pacioli" title="Portrait of Luca Pacioli">Portrait of Luca Pacioli</a></i>, father of accounting, painted by <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_de%27_Barbari" title="Jacopo de' Barbari">Jacopo de' Barbari</a>, 1495, (<a href="/wiki/Museo_di_Capodimonte" title="Museo di Capodimonte">Museo di Capodimonte</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1543,_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica,_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg/170px-1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg" width="170" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg/255px-1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="292" data-file-height="308" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1543,_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica,_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1543' <a href="/wiki/Vesalius" class="mw-redirect" title="Vesalius">Vesalius</a>' studies inspired interest in <a href="/wiki/Human_anatomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Human anatomy">human anatomy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galilee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Galilee.jpg/170px-Galilee.jpg" width="170" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Galilee.jpg/255px-Galilee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Galilee.jpg/340px-Galilee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="651" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galilee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>. Portrait in ink by Renaissance sculptor <a href="/wiki/Leone_Leoni" title="Leone Leoni">Leone Leoni</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The rediscovery of ancient texts and the invention of <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> democratized learning and allowed a faster propagation of more widely distributed ideas. In the first period of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, humanists favoured the study of <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a> over <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a>, and their reverence for classical sources further enshrined the <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic</a> views of the universe. Writing around 1450, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_of_Cusa" title="Nicholas of Cusa">Nicholas Cusanus</a> anticipated the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> worldview of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernicus</a>, but in a philosophical fashion.</p>
<p>Science and art were intermingled in the early Renaissance, with <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> artists such as Leonardo da Vinci making observational drawings of anatomy and nature. Da Vinci set up controlled experiments in water flow, medical dissection, and systematic study of movement and aerodynamics, and he devised principles of research method that led Fritjof Capra to classify him as the "father of modern science".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Other examples of Da Vinci's contribution during this period include machines designed to saw marbles and lift monoliths and new discoveries in acoustics, botany, geology, anatomy and mechanics.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>A suitable environment had developed to question scientific doctrine. The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">discovery</a> in 1492 of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> challenged the classical worldview. The works of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (in geography) and <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> (in medicine) were found to not always match everyday observations. As the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> clashed, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Renaissance" title="Northern Renaissance">Northern Renaissance</a> showed a decisive shift in focus from Aristotelean natural philosophy to chemistry and the biological sciences (botany, anatomy, and medicine).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The willingness to question previously held truths and search for new answers resulted in a period of major scientific advancements.</p>
<p>Some view this as a "<a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>", heralding the beginning of the modern age,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> others as an acceleration of a continuous process stretching from the ancient world to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Significant scientific advances were made during this time by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Copernicus, in <i><a href="/wiki/De_Revolutionibus_Orbium_Coelestium" class="mw-redirect" title="De Revolutionibus Orbium Coelestium">De Revolutionibus</a></i>, posited that the Earth moved around the Sun. <i><a href="/wiki/De_humani_corporis_fabrica" title="De humani corporis fabrica">De humani corporis fabrica</a></i> (<i>On the Workings of the Human Body</i>), by <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a>, gave a new confidence to the role of <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a>, observation, and the <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">mechanistic</a> view of anatomy.<sup id="cite_ref-short-science_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short-science-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another important development was in the <i>process</i> for discovery, the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-short-science_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short-science-62">[62]</a></sup> focusing on <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empirical evidence</a> and the importance of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, while discarding Aristotelian science. Early and influential proponents of these ideas included Copernicus, Galileo, and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The new scientific method led to great contributions in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Applied innovation extended to commerce. At the end of the 15th century <a href="/wiki/Luca_Pacioli" title="Luca Pacioli">Luca Pacioli</a> published the first work on <a href="/wiki/Bookkeeping" title="Bookkeeping">bookkeeping</a>, making him the founder of <a href="/wiki/Accounting" title="Accounting">accounting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jkdiwan_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jkdiwan-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_music" title="Renaissance music">Renaissance music</a></div>
<p>From this changing society emerged a common, unifying musical language, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphonic</a> style of the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Flemish" class="mw-redirect" title="Franco-Flemish">Franco-Flemish</a> school. The development of <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing</a> made distribution of music possible on a wide scale. Demand for music as entertainment and as an activity for educated amateurs increased with the emergence of a bourgeois class. Dissemination of <a href="/wiki/Chanson" title="Chanson">chansons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">motets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">masses</a> throughout Europe coincided with the unification of polyphonic practice into the fluid style that culminated in the second half of the sixteenth century in the work of composers such as <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orlande_de_Lassus" title="Orlande de Lassus">Lassus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1s_Luis_de_Victoria" title="Tomás Luis de Victoria">Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Byrd" title="William Byrd">William Byrd</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg/220px-Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg" width="220" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg/330px-Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg/440px-Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alexander_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander VI">Alexander VI</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Borgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Borgia">Borgia</a> Pope infamous for his corruption</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The new ideals of humanism, although more secular in some aspects, developed against a <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> backdrop, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Renaissance" title="Northern Renaissance">Northern Renaissance</a>. Much, if not most, of the new art was commissioned by or in dedication to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-openuni_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openuni-18">[18]</a></sup> However, the Renaissance had a profound effect on contemporary <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, particularly in the way people perceived the relationship between man and God.<sup id="cite_ref-openuni_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openuni-18">[18]</a></sup> Many of the period's foremost theologians were followers of the humanist method, including <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zwingli" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwingli">Zwingli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clovio_magi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Clovio_magi.jpg/220px-Clovio_magi.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Clovio_magi.jpg/330px-Clovio_magi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Clovio_magi.jpg/440px-Clovio_magi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="468" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clovio_magi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Adoration_of_the_Magi" title="Adoration of the Magi">Adoration of the Magi</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a> adored by the <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Sheba" title="Queen of Sheba">Queen of Sheba</a></i> from the <i><a href="/wiki/Farnese_Hours" title="Farnese Hours">Farnese Hours</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Clovio" title="Giulio Clovio">Giulio Clovio</a> marks <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance#Renaissance_end" title="Italian Renaissance">the end of the Italian Renaissance</a> of <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> together with the <i><a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum">Index Librorum Prohibitorum</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Renaissance began in times of religious turmoil. The late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> was a period of political intrigue surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Papacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a>, in which three men simultaneously claimed to be true <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">Bishop</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Rome" title="Diocese of Rome">Rome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> While the schism was resolved by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> (1414), a resulting reform movement known as <a href="/wiki/Conciliarism" title="Conciliarism">Conciliarism</a> sought to limit the power of the pope. Although the papacy eventually emerged supreme in ecclesiastical matters by the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Council_of_the_Lateran" title="Fifth Council of the Lateran">Fifth Council of the Lateran</a> (1511), it was dogged by continued accusations of corruption, most famously in the person of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a>, who was accused variously of <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a> and fathering four children (most of whom were married off, presumably for the consolidation of power) while a cardinal.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Churchmen such as Erasmus and Luther proposed reform to the Church, often based on humanist <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-openuni_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openuni-18">[18]</a></sup> In October 1517 Luther published the <a href="/wiki/95_Theses" class="mw-redirect" title="95 Theses">95 Theses</a>, challenging papal authority and criticizing its perceived corruption, particularly with regard to instances of sold <a href="/wiki/Indulgences" class="mw-redirect" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[note 1]</a></sup> The 95 Theses led to the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>, a break with the Roman Catholic Church that previously claimed hegemony in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>. Humanism and the Renaissance therefore played a direct role in sparking the Reformation, as well as in many other contemporaneous religious debates and conflicts.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a> came to the papal throne (1534–1549) after the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(1527)" title="Sack of Rome (1527)">sack of Rome in 1527</a>, with uncertainties prevalent in the Catholic Church following the Protestant Reformation. Nicolaus Copernicus dedicated <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> (On the Revolutions of the Celestial Spheres) to Paul III, who became the grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Farnese_(cardinal)" title="Alessandro Farnese (cardinal)">Alessandro Farnese (cardinal)</a>, who had paintings by <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, as well as an important collection of drawings, and who commissioned the masterpiece of <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Clovio" title="Giulio Clovio">Giulio Clovio</a>, arguably the last major <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Farnese_Hours" title="Farnese Hours">Farnese Hours</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-awareness">Self-awareness</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:190px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_historian_Leonardo_Bruni.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/The_historian_Leonardo_Bruni.jpg" width="188" height="257" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="188" data-file-height="257" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_historian_Leonardo_Bruni.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the 15th century, writers, artists, and architects in Italy were well aware of the transformations that were taking place and were using phrases such as <i>modi antichi</i> (in the antique manner) or <i>alle romana et alla antica</i> (in the manner of the Romans and the ancients) to describe their work.
