In the 1330s <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> referred to pre-Christian times as <i>antiqua</i> (ancient) and to the Christian period as <i>nova</i> (new).<sup id="cite_ref-mommsen_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mommsen-71">[70]</a></sup> From Petrarch's Italian perspective, this new period (which included his own time) was an age of national eclipse.<sup id="cite_ref-mommsen_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mommsen-71">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a> was the first to use tripartite <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a> in his <i>History of the Florentine People</i> (1442).<sup id="cite_ref-Hankins_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hankins-72">[71]</a></sup> Bruni's first two periods were based on those of Petrarch, but he added a third period because he believed that Italy was no longer in a state of decline. <a href="/wiki/Flavio_Biondo" title="Flavio Biondo">Flavio Biondo</a> used a similar framework in <i>Decades of History from the Deterioration of the Roman Empire</i> (1439–1453).</p>
<p>Humanist historians argued that contemporary scholarship restored direct links to the classical period, thus bypassing the Medieval period, which they then named for the first time the "<a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>". The term first appears in Latin in 1469 as <i>media tempestas</i> (middle times).<sup id="cite_ref-Albrow_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Albrow-73">[72]</a></sup> The term <i>la rinascita</i> (rebirth) first appeared, however, in its broad sense in <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Vite_de%27_pi%C3%B9_eccellenti_pittori,_scultori,_e_architettori" class="mw-redirect" title="Le Vite de' più eccellenti pittori, scultori, e architettori">Lives of the Artists</a></i>, 1550, revised 1568).<sup id="cite_ref-panofsky_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-panofsky-74">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Vasari divides the age into three phases: the first phase contains <a href="/wiki/Cimabue" title="Cimabue">Cimabue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arnolfo_di_Cambio" title="Arnolfo di Cambio">Arnolfo di Cambio</a>; the second phase contains <a href="/wiki/Masaccio" title="Masaccio">Masaccio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Brunelleschi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a>; the third centers on <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> and culminates with <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>. It was not just the growing awareness of classical antiquity that drove this development, according to Vasari, but also the growing desire to study and imitate nature.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chateau_de_chambord.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Chateau_de_chambord.jpg/220px-Chateau_de_chambord.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Chateau_de_chambord.jpg/330px-Chateau_de_chambord.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Chateau_de_chambord.jpg/440px-Chateau_de_chambord.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chateau_de_chambord.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Chambord" title="Château de Chambord">Château de Chambord</a> (1519–1547), one of the most famous examples of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 15th century, the Renaissance spread rapidly from its birthplace in Florence to the rest of Italy and soon to the rest of Europe. The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> by German printer <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> allowed the rapid transmission of these new ideas. As it spread, its ideas diversified and changed, being adapted to local culture. In the 20th century, scholars began to break the Renaissance into regional and national movements.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Europe">Northern Europe</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Renaissance" title="Northern Renaissance">Northern Renaissance</a></div>
<p>The Renaissance in Northern Europe has been termed the "Northern Renaissance". While Renaissance ideas were moving north from Italy, there was a simultaneous southward spread of some areas of innovation, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_music" title="Renaissance music">music</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-musical-quarterly_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-musical-quarterly-77">[76]</a></sup> The music of the 15th century <a href="/wiki/Burgundian_School" title="Burgundian School">Burgundian School</a> defined the beginning of the Renaissance in music, and the <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphony</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Flemish_School" title="Franco-Flemish School">Netherlanders</a>, as it moved with the musicians themselves into Italy, formed the core of the first true international style in <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a> since the standardization of <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Chant" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Chant">Gregorian Chant</a> in the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-musical-quarterly_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-musical-quarterly-77">[76]</a></sup> The culmination of the Netherlandish school was in the music of the Italian <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">composer</a> <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>. At the end of the 16th century Italy again became a center of musical innovation, with the development of the polychoral style of the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_School_(music)" title="Venetian School (music)">Venetian School</a>, which spread northward into Germany around 1600.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thetriumphofdeath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thetriumphofdeath.jpg/220px-Thetriumphofdeath.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thetriumphofdeath.jpg/330px-Thetriumphofdeath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thetriumphofdeath.jpg/440px-Thetriumphofdeath.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5200" data-file-height="3697" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thetriumphofdeath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pieter_Brueghel_the_Elder" class="mw-redirect" title="Pieter Brueghel the Elder">Pieter Bruegel</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Triumph_of_Death" title="The Triumph of Death">The Triumph of Death</a></i> (c. 1562) reflects the social upheaval and terror that followed the plague that devastated medieval Europe.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The paintings of the Italian Renaissance differed from those of the Northern Renaissance. <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> artists were among the first to paint secular scenes, breaking away from the purely religious art of medieval painters. Northern Renaissance artists initially remained focused on religious subjects, such as the contemporary religious upheaval portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a>. Later, the works of <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Bruegel the Elder">Pieter Bruegel</a> influenced artists to paint scenes of daily life rather than religious or classical themes. It was also during the Northern Renaissance that <a href="/wiki/Flemish_Primitives" class="mw-redirect" title="Flemish Primitives">Flemish</a> brothers <a href="/wiki/Hubert_van_Eyck" title="Hubert van Eyck">Hubert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Eyck" title="Jan van Eyck">Jan van Eyck</a> perfected the <a href="/wiki/Oil_painting" title="Oil painting">oil painting</a> technique, which enabled artists to produce strong colors on a hard surface that could survive for centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> A feature of the Northern Renaissance was its use of the vernacular in place of Latin or Greek, which allowed greater freedom of expression. This movement had started in Italy with the decisive influence of <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a> on the development of vernacular languages; in fact the focus on writing in Italian has neglected a major source of Florentine ideas expressed in Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The spread of the printing press technology boosted the Renaissance in Northern Europe as elsewhere, with Venice becoming a world center of printing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance" title="English Renaissance">English Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shakespeare.jpg/170px-Shakespeare.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shakespeare.jpg/255px-Shakespeare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shakespeare.jpg/340px-Shakespeare.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1943" data-file-height="2489" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"What a piece of work is a man, how noble in reason, how infinite in faculties, in form and moving how express and admirable, in action how like an angel, in apprehension how like a god!" — from <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In England, the sixteenth century marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance" title="English Renaissance">English Renaissance</a> with the work of writers <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_More" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Thomas More">Sir Thomas More</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Philip_Sidney" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Philip Sidney">Sir Philip Sidney</a>, as well as great artists, architects (such as <a href="/wiki/Inigo_Jones" title="Inigo Jones">Inigo Jones</a> who introduced Italianate architecture to England), and composers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Tallis" title="Thomas Tallis">Thomas Tallis</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Taverner" title="John Taverner">John Taverner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Byrd" title="William Byrd">William Byrd</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_Renaissance" title="French Renaissance">French Renaissance</a></div>
<p>The word "Renaissance" is borrowed from the French language, where it means "re-birth". It was first used in the eighteenth century and was later popularized by French <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Jules_Michelet" title="Jules Michelet">Jules Michelet</a> (1798–1874) in his 1855 work, <i>Histoire de France</i> (History of France).<sup id="cite_ref-Michelet.2C_Jules_1847_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michelet.2C_Jules_1847-80">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cronin2011_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cronin2011-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1495 the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> arrived in France, imported by King <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII</a> after his invasion of Italy. A factor that promoted the spread of secularism was the inability of the Church to offer assistance against the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>. <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a> imported Italian art and artists, including <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, and built ornate palaces at great expense. Writers such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">François Rabelais</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Ronsard" title="Pierre de Ronsard">Pierre de Ronsard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joachim_du_Bellay" title="Joachim du Bellay">Joachim du Bellay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a>, painters such as <a href="/wiki/Jean_Clouet" title="Jean Clouet">Jean Clouet</a>, and musicians such as <a href="/wiki/Jean_Mouton" title="Jean Mouton">Jean Mouton</a> also borrowed from the spirit of the Renaissance.</p>
<p>In 1533, a fourteen-year-old <a href="/wiki/Catherine_de%27_Medici" title="Catherine de' Medici">Caterina de' Medici</a> (1519–1589), born in Florence to Lorenzo II de' Medici and Madeleine de la Tour d'Auvergne, married <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_France" title="Henry II of France">Henry II of France</a>, second son of King Francis I and Queen Claude. Though she became famous and infamous for her role in France's religious wars, she made a direct contribution in bringing arts, sciences and music (including the origins of <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>) to the French court from her native Florence.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/German_Renaissance" title="German Renaissance">German Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg/170px-Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg/255px-Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg/340px-Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="2777" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_van_Eyck_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Arnolfini_Portrait" class="mw-redirect" title="The Arnolfini Portrait">The Arnolfini Portrait</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Eyck" title="Jan van Eyck">Jan van Eyck</a>, 1434</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the second half of the 15th century, the Renaissance spirit spread to <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, where the development of the printing press (ca. 1450) and early Renaissance artists such as the painters <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Eyck" title="Jan van Eyck">Jan van Eyck</a> (1395–1441) and <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Bosch" title="Hieronymus Bosch">Hieronymus Bosch</a> (1450–1516) and the composers <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Ockeghem" title="Johannes Ockeghem">Johannes Ockeghem</a> (1410–1497), <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Obrecht" title="Jacob Obrecht">Jacob Obrecht</a> (1457–1505) and <a href="/wiki/Josquin_des_Prez" title="Josquin des Prez">Josquin des Prez</a> (1455–1521) predated the influence from Italy. In the early Protestant areas of the country <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> became closely linked to the turmoil of the Protestant Reformation, and the art and writing of the <a href="/wiki/German_Renaissance" title="German Renaissance">German Renaissance</a> frequently reflected this dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> However, the gothic style and medieval scholastic philosophy remained exclusively until the turn of the 16th century. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> (ruling 1493–1519) was the first truly Renaissance monarch of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance in the Netherlands">Renaissance in the Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_and_Flemish_Renaissance_painting" title="Dutch and Flemish Renaissance painting">Dutch and Flemish Renaissance painting</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holbein-erasmus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/170px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/255px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/340px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="809" data-file-height="1145" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holbein-erasmus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Erasmus_of_Rotterdam" class="mw-redirect" title="Erasmus of Rotterdam">Erasmus of Rotterdam</a> in 1523, as depicted by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Culture in the Netherlands at the end of the 15th century was influenced by the Italian Renaissance through trade via <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a>, which made Flanders wealthy. Its nobles commissioned artists who became known across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Heughebaert_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heughebaert-83">[82]</a></sup> In science, the <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomist</a> <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a> led the way; in <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a>'s map assisted explorers and navigators. In art, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_and_Flemish_Renaissance_painting" title="Dutch and Flemish Renaissance painting">Dutch and Flemish Renaissance painting</a> ranged from the strange work of <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Bosch" title="Hieronymus Bosch">Hieronymus Bosch</a><sup id="cite_ref-Janson_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janson-84">[83]</a></sup> to the everyday life depictions of <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Brueghel_the_Elder" class="mw-redirect" title="Pieter Brueghel the Elder">Pieter Brueghel the Elder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heughebaert_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heughebaert-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Renaissance" title="Spanish Renaissance">Spanish Renaissance</a></div>
<p>The Renaissance arrived in the Iberian peninsula through the Mediterranean possessions of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Aragonese Crown</a> and the city of <a href="/wiki/Valencia_(city_in_Spain)" class="mw-redirect" title="Valencia (city in Spain)">Valencia</a>. Many early Spanish Renaissance writers come from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">Kingdom of Aragon</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Ausi%C3%A0s_March" title="Ausiàs March">Ausiàs March</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joanot_Martorell" title="Joanot Martorell">Joanot Martorell</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Kingdom of Castile</a>, the early Renaissance was heavily influenced by the Italian humanism, starting with writers and poets such as <a href="/wiki/%C3%8D%C3%B1igo_L%C3%B3pez_de_Mendoza,_marqu%C3%A9s_de_Santillana" class="mw-redirect" title="Íñigo López de Mendoza, marqués de Santillana">the Marquis of Santillana</a>, who introduced the new Italian poetry to Spain in the early 15th century. Other writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Manrique" title="Jorge Manrique">Jorge Manrique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Rojas" title="Fernando de Rojas">Fernando de Rojas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_del_Encina" title="Juan del Encina">Juan del Encina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Bosc%C3%A1n_Almog%C3%A1ver" title="Juan Boscán Almogáver">Juan Boscán Almogáver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Garcilaso_de_la_Vega_(poet)" title="Garcilaso de la Vega (poet)">Garcilaso de la Vega</a>, kept a close resemblance to the Italian canon. <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Miguel de Cervantes</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">masterpiece</a> <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">Don Quixote</a> is credited as the first Western novel. Renaissance humanism flourished in the early 16th century, with influential writers such as philosopher <a href="/wiki/Juan_Luis_Vives" title="Juan Luis Vives">Juan Luis Vives</a>, grammarian <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Nebrija" title="Antonio de Nebrija">Antonio de Nebrija</a> and natural historian <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Mex%C3%ADa" title="Pedro Mexía">Pedro de Mexía</a>.</p>
<p>Later Spanish Renaissance tended towards religious themes and mysticism, with poets such as <a href="/wiki/Luis_Ponce_de_Le%C3%B3n" class="mw-redirect" title="Luis Ponce de León">fray Luis de León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teresa_of_%C3%81vila" title="Teresa of Ávila">Teresa of Ávila</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_of_the_Cross" title="John of the Cross">John of the Cross</a>, and treated issues related to the exploration of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, with chroniclers and writers such as <a href="/wiki/Inca_Garcilaso_de_la_Vega" title="Inca Garcilaso de la Vega">Inca Garcilaso de la Vega</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">Bartolomé de las Casas</a>, giving rise to a body of work, now known as <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Renaissance_literature" title="Spanish Renaissance literature">Spanish Renaissance literature</a>. The late Renaissance in Spain produced artists such as <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">El Greco</a> and composers such as <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1s_Luis_de_Victoria" title="Tomás Luis de Victoria">Tomás Luis de Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Cabez%C3%B3n" title="Antonio de Cabezón">Antonio de Cabezón</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Renaissance" title="Portuguese Renaissance">Portuguese Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GraoVasco1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/GraoVasco1.jpg/220px-GraoVasco1.jpg" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/GraoVasco1.jpg/330px-GraoVasco1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/GraoVasco1.jpg/440px-GraoVasco1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="637" data-file-height="590" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GraoVasco1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>São Pedro Papa</i>, 1530-1535, by <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A3o_Vasco" title="Grão Vasco">Grão Vasco Fernandes</a>. A pinnacle piece from when the Portuguese Renaissance had considerable external influence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although Italian Renaissance had a modest impact in Portuguese arts, Portugal was influential in broadening the European worldview,<sup id="cite_ref-JCBL_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCBL-85">[84]</a></sup> stimulating humanist inquiry. Renaissance arrived through the influence of wealthy Italian and Flemish merchants who invested in the profitable commerce overseas. As the pioneer headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">European exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> flourished in the late 15th century, attracting experts who made several breakthroughs in mathematics, astronomy and naval technology, including <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Nunes" title="Pedro Nunes">Pedro Nunes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_de_Castro" title="João de Castro">João de Castro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">Abraham Zacuto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Behaim" title="Martin Behaim">Martin Behaim</a>. Cartographers <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Reinel" title="Pedro Reinel">Pedro Reinel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lopo_Homem" title="Lopo Homem">Lopo Homem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Est%C3%AAv%C3%A3o_Gomes" title="Estêvão Gomes">Estêvão Gomes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diogo_Ribeiro" title="Diogo Ribeiro">Diogo Ribeiro</a> made crucial advances in mapping the world. Apothecary <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A9_Pires" title="Tomé Pires">Tomé Pires</a> and physicians <a href="/wiki/Garcia_de_Orta" title="Garcia de Orta">Garcia de Orta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3v%C3%A3o_da_Costa" title="Cristóvão da Costa">Cristóvão da Costa</a> collected and published works on plants and medicines, soon translated by Flemish pioneer botanist <a href="/wiki/Carolus_Clusius" title="Carolus Clusius">Carolus Clusius</a>.</p>
<p>In architecture, the huge profits of the <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> financed a sumptuous composite style in the first decades of the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Manueline" title="Manueline">Manueline</a>, incorporating maritime elements.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> The primary painters were <a href="/wiki/Nuno_Gon%C3%A7alves" title="Nuno Gonçalves">Nuno Gonçalves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greg%C3%B3rio_Lopes" title="Gregório Lopes">Gregório Lopes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A3o_Vasco" title="Grão Vasco">Vasco Fernandes</a>. In music, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Escobar" title="Pedro de Escobar">Pedro de Escobar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Lobo" title="Duarte Lobo">Duarte Lobo</a> produced four songbooks, including the <a href="/wiki/Cancioneiro_de_Elvas" title="Cancioneiro de Elvas">Cancioneiro de Elvas</a>. In literature, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1_de_Miranda" class="mw-redirect" title="Sá de Miranda">Sá de Miranda</a> introduced Italian forms of verse. <a href="/wiki/Bernardim_Ribeiro" title="Bernardim Ribeiro">Bernardim Ribeiro</a> developed <a href="/wiki/Pastoral#Pastoral_romances" title="Pastoral">pastoral romance</a>, plays by <a href="/wiki/Gil_Vicente" title="Gil Vicente">Gil Vicente</a> fused it with popular culture, reporting the changing times, and <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_de_Cam%C3%B5es" title="Luís de Camões">Luís de Camões</a> inscribed the Portuguese feats overseas in the epic poem <a href="/wiki/Os_Lus%C3%ADadas" title="Os Lusíadas">Os Lusíadas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Travel_literature" title="Travel literature">Travel literature</a> especially flourished: <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_de_Barros" title="João de Barros">João de Barros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_Lopes_de_Castanheda" title="Fernão Lopes de Castanheda">Castanheda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Galv%C3%A3o" title="António Galvão">António Galvão</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_Correia" title="Gaspar Correia">Gaspar Correia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_Mendes_Pinto" title="Fernão Mendes Pinto">Fernão Mendes Pinto</a>, among others, described new lands and were translated and spread with the new printing press.<sup id="cite_ref-JCBL_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCBL-85">[84]</a></sup> After joining the Portuguese exploration of Brazil in 1500, <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a> coined the term <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bergin_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergin-87">[86]</a></sup> in his letters to <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_di_Pierfrancesco_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo di Pierfrancesco de' Medici">Lorenzo di Pierfrancesco de' Medici</a>.</p>
<p>The intense international exchange produced several cosmopolitan humanist scholars, including <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Holanda" title="Francisco de Holanda">Francisco de Holanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_de_Resende" title="André de Resende">André de Resende</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dami%C3%A3o_de_G%C3%B3is" title="Damião de Góis">Damião de Góis</a>, a friend of Erasmus who wrote with rare independence on the reign of King <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_of_Portugal" title="Manuel I of Portugal">Manuel I</a>. <a href="/wiki/Diogo_de_Gouveia" title="Diogo de Gouveia">Diogo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_de_Gouveia" title="André de Gouveia">André de Gouveia</a> made relevant teaching reforms via France. Foreign news and products in the Portuguese <a href="/wiki/Factory_(trading_post)" title="Factory (trading post)">factory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> attracted the interest of Thomas More<sup id="cite_ref-Bietenholz_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bietenholz-88">[87]</a></sup> and Dürer to the wider world.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> There, profits and know-how helped nurture the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch Renaissance">Dutch Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Golden Age</a>, especially after the arrival of the wealthy cultured Jewish community expelled from Portugal.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungary">Hungary</span></h3>
<p>After Italy, Hungary was the first European country where the renaissance appeared.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> The Renaissance style came directly from Italy during the <a href="/wiki/Quattrocento" title="Quattrocento">Quattrocento</a> to Hungary first in the Central European region, thanks to the development of early Hungarian-Italian relationships – not only in dynastic connections, but also in cultural, humanistic and commercial relations – growing in strength from the 14th century. The relationship between Hungarian and Italian Gothic styles was a second reason – exaggerated breakthrough of walls is avoided, preferring clean and light structures. Large-scale building schemes provided ample and long term work for the artists, for example, the building of the Friss (New) Castle in Buda, the castles of Visegrád, Tata and Várpalota. In Sigismund's court there were patrons such as Pipo Spano, a descendant of the Scolari family of Florence, who invited Manetto Ammanatini and Masolino da Pannicale to Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The new Italian trend combined with existing national traditions to create a particular local Renaissance art. Acceptance of Renaissance art was furthered by the continuous arrival of humanist thought in the country. Many young Hungarians studying at Italian universities came closer to the <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florentine</a> humanist center, so a direct connection with <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> evolved. The growing number of Italian traders moving to Hungary, specially to <a href="/wiki/Buda" title="Buda">Buda</a>, helped this process. New thoughts were carried by the humanist prelates, among them <a href="/wiki/Vit%C3%A9z_J%C3%A1nos" class="mw-redirect" title="Vitéz János">Vitéz János</a>, archbishop of <a href="/wiki/Esztergom" title="Esztergom">Esztergom</a>, one of the founders of Hungarian humanism.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> During the long reign of emperor <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_of_Luxemburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Sigismund of Luxemburg">Sigismund of Luxemburg</a> the <a href="/wiki/Buda_Castle" title="Buda Castle">Royal Castle of Buda</a> became probably the largest <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic</a> palace of the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Corvinus" title="Matthias Corvinus">Matthias Corvinus</a> (r. 1458–1490) rebuilt the palace in early Renaissance style and further expanded it.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the marriage in 1476 of King Matthias to <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_of_Naples" title="Beatrice of Naples">Beatrice of Naples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buda" title="Buda">Buda</a> became one of the most important artistic centres of the Renaissance north of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-czigany_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-czigany-95">[94]</a></sup> The most important humanists living in Matthias' court were <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Bonfini" title="Antonio Bonfini">Antonio Bonfini</a> and the famous Hungarian poet <a href="/wiki/Janus_Pannonius" title="Janus Pannonius">Janus Pannonius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-czigany_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-czigany-95">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Hess" title="András Hess">András Hess</a> set up a printing press in Buda in 1472. Matthias Corvinus's library, the <i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_Corviniana" title="Bibliotheca Corviniana">Bibliotheca Corviniana</a></i>, was Europe's greatest collections of secular books: historical chronicles, philosophic and scientific works in the 15th century. His library was second only in size to the Vatican Library. (However, the Vatican Library mainly contained Bibles and religious materials.)<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1489, Bartolomeo della Fonte of Florence wrote that Lorenzo de' Medici founded his own Greek-Latin library encouraged by the example of the Hungarian king. Corvinus's library is part of UNESCO World Heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> Other important figures of Hungarian Renaissance include <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1lint_Balassi" title="Bálint Balassi">Bálint Balassi</a> (poet), <a href="/wiki/Sebesty%C3%A9n_Tin%C3%B3di_Lantos" title="Sebestyén Tinódi Lantos">Sebestyén Tinódi Lantos</a> (poet), <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1lint_Bakfark" title="Bálint Bakfark">Bálint Bakfark</a> (composer and lutenist), and <a href="/wiki/Master_MS" title="Master MS">Master MS</a> (fresco painter).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Poland" title="Renaissance in Poland">Renaissance in Poland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ayuntamiento,_Poznan,_Polonia,_2014-09-18,_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ayuntamiento%2C_Poznan%2C_Polonia%2C_2014-09-18%2C_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg/170px-Ayuntamiento%2C_Poznan%2C_Polonia%2C_2014-09-18%2C_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg" width="170" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ayuntamiento%2C_Poznan%2C_Polonia%2C_2014-09-18%2C_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg/255px-Ayuntamiento%2C_Poznan%2C_Polonia%2C_2014-09-18%2C_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ayuntamiento%2C_Poznan%2C_Polonia%2C_2014-09-18%2C_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg/340px-Ayuntamiento%2C_Poznan%2C_Polonia%2C_2014-09-18%2C_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3088" data-file-height="5064" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ayuntamiento,_Poznan,_Polonia,_2014-09-18,_DD_67-72_HDR.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84_Town_Hall" title="Poznań Town Hall">Poznań Town Hall</a> rebuilt from the Gothic style by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Batista_di_Quadro" class="mw-redirect" title="Giovanni Batista di Quadro">Giovanni Batista di Quadro</a> (1550–1555)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An early Italian humanist who came to <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> in the mid-15th century was <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Buonaccorsi" title="Filippo Buonaccorsi">Filippo Buonaccorsi</a>. Many Italian artists came to Poland with <a href="/wiki/Bona_Sforza" title="Bona Sforza">Bona Sforza</a> of Milan, when she married King <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Sigismund I the Old</a> in 1518.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> This was supported by temporarily strengthened monarchies in both areas, as well as by newly established universities.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> The Polish Renaissance lasted from the late 15th to the late 16th century and was the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Golden_Age" title="Polish Golden Age">Golden Age</a> of <a href="/wiki/Polish_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish culture">Polish culture</a>. Ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Jagiellon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Jagiellon dynasty">Jagiellon dynasty</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)">Kingdom of Poland</a> (from 1569 known as the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>) actively participated in the broad European Renaissance. The multi-national Polish state experienced a substantial period of cultural growth thanks in part to a century without major wars – aside from conflicts in the sparsely populated eastern and southern borderlands. The <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a> spread peacefully throughout the country (giving rise to the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Brethren" title="Polish Brethren">Polish Brethren</a>), while living conditions improved, cities grew, and exports of agricultural products enriched the population, especially the nobility (<i><a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">szlachta</a></i>) who gained dominance in the new political system of <a href="/wiki/Golden_Liberty" title="Golden Liberty">Golden Liberty</a>. The Polish Renaissance architecture has three periods of development.</p>
<p>The greatest monument of this style in the territory of the former <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Pomerania" title="Duchy of Pomerania">Duchy of Pomerania</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Ducal_Castle,_Szczecin" title="Ducal Castle, Szczecin">Ducal Castle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Szczecin</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h3>
<p>Renaissance trends from Italy and Central Europe influenced Russia in many ways. Their influence was rather limited, however, due to the large distances between Russia and the main European cultural centers and the strong adherence of Russians to their <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Orthodox traditions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">Byzantine legacy</a>.</p>
<p>Prince <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Ivan III">Ivan III</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a> to <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> by inviting a number of architects from <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, who brought new construction techniques and some Renaissance style elements with them, while in general following the traditional designs of <a href="/wiki/Russian_architecture" title="Russian architecture">Russian architecture</a>. In 1475 the Bolognese architect <a href="/wiki/Aristotele_Fioravanti" title="Aristotele Fioravanti">Aristotele Fioravanti</a> came to rebuild the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_the_Dormition" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of the Dormition">Cathedral of the Dormition</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a>, which had been damaged in an earthquake. Fioravanti was given the 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Assumption_Cathedral_in_Vladimir" class="mw-redirect" title="Assumption Cathedral in Vladimir">Vladimir Cathedral</a> as a model, and he produced a design combining traditional Russian style with a Renaissance sense of spaciousness, proportion and symmetry.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg/220px-Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg/330px-Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg/440px-Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palais_%C3%A0_facettes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Facets" title="Palace of Facets">Palace of Facets</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Square,_Moscow" title="Cathedral Square, Moscow">Cathedral Square</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1485 Ivan III commissioned the building of the royal residence, <a href="/wiki/Terem_Palace" title="Terem Palace">Terem Palace</a>, within the Kremlin, with <a href="/wiki/Aloisio_da_Milano" title="Aloisio da Milano">Aloisio da Milano</a> as the architect of the first three floors. He and other Italian architects also contributed to the construction of the Kremlin walls and towers. The small banquet hall of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Tsar" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Tsar">Russian Tsars</a>, called the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Facets" title="Palace of Facets">Palace of Facets</a> because of its facetted upper story, is the work of two Italians, <a href="/wiki/Marco_Ruffo" title="Marco Ruffo">Marco Ruffo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Solario" class="mw-redirect" title="Pietro Solario">Pietro Solario</a>, and shows a more Italian style. In 1505, an Italian known in Russia as <a href="/wiki/Aleviz_Novyi" class="mw-redirect" title="Aleviz Novyi">Aleviz Novyi</a> or Aleviz Fryazin arrived in Moscow. He may have been the Venetian sculptor, Alevisio Lamberti da Montagne. He built 12 churches for Ivan III, including the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_the_Archangel" title="Cathedral of the Archangel">Cathedral of the Archangel</a>, a building remarkable for the successful blending of Russian tradition, Orthodox requirements and Renaissance style. It is believed that the Cathedral of the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Peter" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Peter">Metropolitan Peter</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vysokopetrovsky_Monastery" title="Vysokopetrovsky Monastery">Vysokopetrovsky Monastery</a>, another work of Aleviz Novyi, later served as an inspiration for the so-called <i>octagon-on-tetragon</i> architectural form in the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Baroque" class="mw-redirect" title="Moscow Baroque">Moscow Baroque</a> of the late 17th century.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg/170px-Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg" width="170" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg/255px-Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg/340px-Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1647" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Karp_Zolotaryov_Theotokos_Late_17th_century.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">The Child</a></i>, the late 17th century <a href="/wiki/Russian_icons" title="Russian icons">Russian icon</a> by <a href="/wiki/Karp_Zolotaryov" title="Karp Zolotaryov">Karp Zolotaryov</a>, with notably realistic depiction of faces and clothing.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between the early 16th and the late 17th centuries, an original tradition of stone <a href="/wiki/Tented_roof" title="Tented roof">tented roof</a> architecture developed in Russia. It was quite unique and different from the contemporary Renaissance architecture elsewhere in Europe, though some research terms the style 'Russian Gothic' and compares it with the European <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic architecture</a> of the earlier period. The Italians, with their advanced technology, may have influenced the invention of the stone tented roof (the wooden tents were known in Russia and Europe long before). According to one hypothesis, an Italian architect called <a href="/wiki/Petrok_Maly" title="Petrok Maly">Petrok Maly</a> may have been an author of the Ascension Church in <a href="/wiki/Kolomenskoye" title="Kolomenskoye">Kolomenskoye</a>, one of the earliest and most prominent tented roof churches.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 17th century the influence of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_painting" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance painting">Renaissance painting</a> resulted in <a href="/wiki/Russian_icons" title="Russian icons">Russian icons</a> becoming slightly more realistic, while still following most of the old icon painting <a href="/wiki/Aesthetic_canon" title="Aesthetic canon">canons</a>, as seen in the works of <a href="/wiki/Bogdan_Saltanov" title="Bogdan Saltanov">Bogdan Saltanov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Ushakov" title="Simon Ushakov">Simon Ushakov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gury_Nikitin" title="Gury Nikitin">Gury Nikitin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karp_Zolotaryov" title="Karp Zolotaryov">Karp Zolotaryov</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Russian_artists" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian artists">Russian artists</a> of the era. Gradually the new type of secular portrait painting appeared, called <i>parsúna</i> (from "persona" – person), which was transitional style between abstract iconographics and real paintings.</p>
<p>In the mid 16th-century Russians adopted <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> from Central Europe, with <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Fyodorov_(printer)" title="Ivan Fyodorov (printer)">Ivan Fyodorov</a> being the first known Russian printer. In the 17th century printing became widespread, and <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcuts</a> became especially popular. That led to the development of a special form of <a href="/wiki/Folk_art" title="Folk art">folk art</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Lubok" title="Lubok">lubok</a> printing, which persisted in Russia well into the 19th century.</p>
<p>A number of technologies from the European Renaissance period were adopted by Russia rather early and subsequently perfected to become a part of a strong domestic tradition. Mostly these were military technologies, such as <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a> <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">casting</a> adopted by at least the 15th century. The <a href="/wiki/Tsar_Cannon" title="Tsar Cannon">Tsar Cannon</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cannon_by_caliber" class="mw-redirect" title="List of largest cannon by caliber">world's largest bombard by caliber</a>, is a masterpiece of Russian cannon making. It was cast in 1586 by <a href="/wiki/Andrey_Chokhov" title="Andrey Chokhov">Andrey Chokhov</a> and is notable for its rich, decorative <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a>. Another technology, that according to one hypothesis originally was brought from Europe by the <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a>, resulted in the development of <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>, the national beverage of Russia. As early as 1386 <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoese</a> ambassadors brought the first aqua vitae ("water of life") to <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> and presented it to <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duke</a> <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Donskoy" title="Dmitry Donskoy">Dmitry Donskoy</a>. The Genoese likely developed this beverage with the help of the <a href="/wiki/Alchemist" class="mw-redirect" title="Alchemist">alchemists</a> of <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, who used an <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a>-invented <a href="/wiki/Distillation_apparatus" class="mw-redirect" title="Distillation apparatus">distillation apparatus</a> to convert <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a> <a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a> into <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>. A Moscovite monk called <a href="/wiki/Isidore_(inventor)" title="Isidore (inventor)">Isidore</a> used this technology to produce the first original <a href="/wiki/Russian_vodka" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian vodka">Russian vodka</a> c. 1430.<sup id="cite_ref-pohlebkin_history_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pohlebkin_history-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_countries">Further countries</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Croatia" title="Renaissance in Croatia">Renaissance in Croatia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Scotland" title="Renaissance in Scotland">Renaissance in Scotland</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conception">Conception</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Vite.jpg/220px-Vite.jpg" width="220" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Vite.jpg/330px-Vite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Vite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="568" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A cover of the <i>Lives of the Artists</i> by <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Italian <a href="/wiki/Artist" title="Artist">artist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Critic" title="Critic">critic</a> <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> (1511–1574) first used the term <i>rinascita</i> retrospectively in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lives_of_the_Artists" class="mw-redirect" title="The Lives of the Artists">The Lives of the Artists</a></i> (published 1550). In the book Vasari attempted to define what he described as a break with the barbarities of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">gothic art</a>: the arts (he held) had fallen into decay with the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and only the <a href="/wiki/Tuscana" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuscana">Tuscan</a> artists, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Cimabue" title="Cimabue">Cimabue</a> (1240–1301) and <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a> (1267–1337) began to reverse this decline in the arts. Vasari saw antique art as central to the rebirth of Italian art.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, only in the 19th century did the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> word <i>Renaissance</i> achieve popularity in describing the self-conscious cultural movement based on revival of Roman models that began in the late-13th century. French <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Jules_Michelet" title="Jules Michelet">Jules Michelet</a> (1798–1874) defined "The Renaissance" in his 1855 work <i>Histoire de France</i> as an entire historical period, whereas previously it had been used in a more limited sense.<sup id="cite_ref-mur_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mur-16">[16]</a></sup> For Michelet, the Renaissance was more a development in science than in art and culture. He asserted that it spanned the period from <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Columbus</a> to Copernicus to <a href="/wiki/Galileo" class="mw-redirect" title="Galileo">Galileo</a>; that is, from the end of the 15th century to the middle of the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Michelet.2C_Jules_1847_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michelet.2C_Jules_1847-80">[79]</a></sup> Moreover, Michelet distinguished between what he called, "the bizarre and monstrous" quality of the Middle Ages and the <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> values that he, as a vocal <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republican</a>, chose to see in its character.<sup id="cite_ref-brotton_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brotton-10">[10]</a></sup> A French nationalist, Michelet also sought to claim the Renaissance as a French movement.<sup id="cite_ref-brotton_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brotton-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Burckhardt" title="Jacob Burckhardt">Jacob Burckhardt</a> (1818–1897) in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Civilization_of_the_Renaissance_in_Italy" title="The Civilization of the Renaissance in Italy">The Civilization of the Renaissance in Italy</a></i> (1860), by contrast, defined the Renaissance as the period between Giotto and <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> in Italy, that is, the 14th to mid-16th centuries. He saw in the Renaissance the emergence of the modern spirit of <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individuality</a>, which the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> had stifled.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> His book was widely read and became influential in the development of the modern interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> However, Buckhardt has been accused<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2013)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> of setting forth a linear <a href="/wiki/Whig_history" title="Whig history">Whiggish</a> view of history in seeing the Renaissance as the origin of the modern world.<sup id="cite_ref-starn_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starn-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>More recently, some historians have been much less keen to define the Renaissance as a historical age, or even as a coherent cultural movement. The historian Randolph Starn, of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California_Berkeley" class="mw-redirect" title="University of California Berkeley">University of California Berkeley</a>, stated in 1998:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"Rather than a period with definitive beginnings and endings and consistent content in between, the Renaissance can be (and occasionally has been) seen as a movement of practices and ideas to which specific groups and identifiable persons variously responded in different times and places. It would be in this sense a network of diverse, sometimes converging, sometimes conflicting cultures, not a single, time-bound culture".<sup id="cite_ref-starn_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starn-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debates_about_progress">Debates about progress</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Continuity_thesis" title="Continuity thesis">Continuity thesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francois_Dubois_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Francois_Dubois_001.jpg/220px-Francois_Dubois_001.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Francois_Dubois_001.jpg/330px-Francois_Dubois_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Francois_Dubois_001.jpg/440px-Francois_Dubois_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1331" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francois_Dubois_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of the <a href="/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Day_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Bartholomew's Day Massacre">St. Bartholomew's Day Massacre</a>, an event in the <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">French Wars of Religion</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois" title="François Dubois">François Dubois</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is debate about the extent to which the Renaissance improved on the culture of the Middle Ages. Both Michelet and Burckhardt were keen to describe the progress made in the Renaissance towards the <a href="/wiki/Modern_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern age">modern age</a>. Burckhardt likened the change to a veil being removed from man's eyes, allowing him to see clearly.<sup id="cite_ref-burckhardt-individual_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burckhardt-individual-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In the Middle Ages both sides of human consciousness – that which was turned within as that which was turned without – lay dreaming or half awake beneath a common veil. The veil was woven of faith, illusion, and childish prepossession, through which the world and history were seen clad in strange hues.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Jacob Burckhardt, <i>The Civilization of the Renaissance in Italy</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>On the other hand, many historians now point out that most of the negative social factors popularly associated with the medieval period – poverty, warfare, religious and political persecution, for example – seem to have worsened in this era, which saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Machiavellian politics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">Wars of Religion</a>, the corrupt <a href="/wiki/Borgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Borgia">Borgia</a> Popes, and the intensified <a href="/wiki/Witch-hunt" title="Witch-hunt">witch-hunts</a> of the 16th century. Many people who lived during the Renaissance did not view it as the "<a href="/wiki/Golden_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden age">golden age</a>" imagined by certain 19th-century authors, but were concerned by these social maladies.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Significantly, though, the artists, writers, and patrons involved in the cultural movements in question believed they were living in a new era that was a clean break from the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-panofsky_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-panofsky-74">[73]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">Marxist historians</a> prefer to describe the Renaissance in material terms, holding the view that the changes in art, literature, and philosophy were part of a general economic trend from <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Bourgeois" class="mw-redirect" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> class with leisure time to devote to the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johan_Huizinga" title="Johan Huizinga">Johan Huizinga</a> (1872–1945) acknowledged the existence of the Renaissance but questioned whether it was a positive change. In his book <i>The Waning of the Middle Ages</i>, he argued that the Renaissance was a period of decline from the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, destroying much that was important.<sup id="cite_ref-huizinga_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huizinga-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin language</a>, for instance, had evolved greatly from the classical period and was still a living language used in the church and elsewhere. The Renaissance obsession with classical purity halted its further evolution and saw Latin revert to its classical form. Robert S. Lopez has contended that it was a period of deep <a href="/wiki/Economic_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic recession">economic recession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/George_Sarton" title="George Sarton">George Sarton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Thorndike" title="Lynn Thorndike">Lynn Thorndike</a> have both argued that <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> progress was perhaps less original than has traditionally been supposed.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Finally, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Kelly" title="Joan Kelly">Joan Kelly</a> argued that the Renaissance led to greater gender dichotomy, lessening the agency women had had during the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some historians have begun to consider the word <i>Renaissance</i> to be unnecessarily loaded, implying an unambiguously positive rebirth from the supposedly more primitive "<a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a>", the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. Most historians now prefer to use the term "<a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern Europe">early modern</a>" for this period, a more neutral designation that highlights the period as a transitional one between the Middle Ages and the modern era.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Others such as Roger Osborne have come to consider the Italian Renaissance as a repository of the myths and ideals of western history in general, and instead of rebirth of ancient ideas as a period of great innovation.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Renaissances">Other Renaissances</span></h2>
<p>The term <i>Renaissance</i> has also been used to define periods outside of the 15th and 16th centuries. <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Haskins" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles H. Haskins">Charles H. Haskins</a> (1870–1937), for example, made a case for a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> Other historians have argued for a <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> in the 8th and 9th centuries, and still later for an <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_Renaissance" title="Ottonian Renaissance">Ottonian Renaissance</a> in the 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> Other periods of cultural rebirth have also been termed "renaissances", such as the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengal Renaissance">Bengal Renaissance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Renaissance" title="Tamil Renaissance">Tamil Renaissance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal_Bhasa_renaissance" title="Nepal Bhasa renaissance">Nepal Bhasa renaissance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Nahda" title="Al-Nahda">al-Nahda</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Harlem_Renaissance" title="Harlem Renaissance">Harlem Renaissance</a>.</p>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> cites W Dyce and C H Wilson’s <i>Letter to Lord Meadowbank</i> (1837): "A style possessing many points of rude resemblance with the more elegant and refined character of the art of the renaissance in Italy." <li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Capra, Fritjof, <i>The Science of Leonardo; Inside the Mind of the Great Genius of the Renaissance</i>, New York, Doubleday, 2007. Exhaustive 2007 study by Fritjof Capra shows that Leonardo was a much greater scientist than previously thought, and not just an inventor. Leonardo was innovative in science theory and in conducting actual science practice. In Capra's detailed assessment of many surviving manuscripts, Leonardo's science in tune with holistic non-mechanistic and non-reductive approaches to science, which are becoming popular today.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Joseph Ben-David wrote:</span>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="reference-text">Rapid accumulation of knowledge, which has characterized the development of science since the 17th century, had never occurred before that time. The new kind of scientific activity emerged only in a few countries of Western Europe, and it was restricted to that small area for about two hundred years. (Since the 19th century, scientific knowledge has been assimilated by the rest of the world).</span></p>
</blockquote>
